Military\nHelicopters\t\n\nG\n\nr\t\n\n2-4\n\n2.1\n\nv\n\n,y\n\n^a\t\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nf\n\nI\n\ny\n\n_\n\nFor at least a decade following the end of World War II the USA was\npreeminent in the field of helicopter design, development and production, and\nworld markets for both military and civil helicopters were dominated by the\nUSA.\nOver the past decade, however, this dominance has been steadily\neroded by a determined effort by -foreign manufacturers, European companies, in\n\n4\t\n\nparticular, to supply their own domestic markets and also to penetrate export\n.\t\n\nmarkets, including the USA.\nTo assess the extent of the threat to the U.S. helicopter industry\nposed by these developments, this report first collates available data on\nU.S. and world markets for civil and military respectively.\t\n\nData are\'_presented\n\nr\n\nin1both graphic and tabular form and cover the past history of production and\nmarkets and, where forecasts are available, anticipated future trends. \t\n\nThe data\n\nare discussed on an item-by-item basis and inferences are drawn in as much\n\n;-\t\n\ndepth as appears to be justified.\nThe employment levels of.the major manufacturers over a; recent threeyear period are compared to identify possible growth trends.\n`\t\n\nI-T\n\nw\n\nII. CIVIL HELICOPTER MARKET\n\nWorld civil helicopter production data are very limited.\t\nIndustries\t\n\nAerospace\n\nAssociation\t (AIA)\t publishes\' production data on U.S. manufacturers\n\nHowever, there is no single source of information on foreign production.\t\n\nThe\n\nCommission of the European Communities has recently (early 1970\'s) become\ninterested in the competitive position of the European aerospace industry and\nas a result is producing an annual\t report, The European Aerospace IndustnL\nPoi,ition and Figures, which contains some information on the helicopter industry.\nSince all\t four major foreign helicopter producers, Aerospatiale (France),\nAgusta\t (Italy), Messerschmitt-Boelkow-Blohm (MBB) \t (Germany), and Westland\n(United Kingdom), are European and members of the European Economic Community,\nthis annual report has the potential of becoming a good source of information.\nIndividual helicopter manufacturers periodi , cally perform market - studies but\nthese studies are proprietary in nature.\nFigure 2.1 presents a summary of the projections available on the\nworld civil helicopter market. \t\n\nThe actual world output (1965-75) of helicopters\n\nis estimated by summing the AIA data on U.S. manufacturers\'\t output and the\nsurvey data of Wayne Hitchcock on the foreign manufacturers\' output. \t\non\t\n\nBased\n\nthis estimate 11,467 civil helicopters were produced from 1965 through\n\n2-1\n\nP\n\n2800\n2400\n\nGE(WORLD)e\n,-(10,000 LBS. WORLD CIVIL HELICOPTER, PRODUCTION, 1965-90\n(UNITS)\n17\n\n2-2\n\nJ\n\n\t\ni ,.\t\n\nb\t\n\n__.s^__..._..^.^.^. ^t^ ^^ ^v_^\n\n+\n\n_z^-^"\xc2\xb0r--x\'x^pr,:r^n"_\'.^\'^,_,pm^_\n\'\n.__..\n\n^\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n_..::_..>.^>....\nT--\n\n3^\n\t\n\n9\n\n1915, 6,680 by U.S. manufacturers and 4,787 by foreign manufacturers. None\nr\t\n\nof the forecasts of civil helicopter production done by (1) Defense Marketing\nj\t\n\nu\t\n\nServices (OMS),(2) Forecast Associates,(3) General Electric,and (4) The Federal\nAviation Administration are quite satisfactory. The DMS forecast was calculated by summing the forecasts for individual models which are listed in\n\nw\t\n\nTable 2.1. Absent from this list is any civil production by Agusta and MBB.\nThe Forecast Associates forecast excludes all piston powered helicopters such\nas those produced by Hughes and Enstrom. One recent estimate suggested that\nannual piston powered helicopter production accounted for about 20 percent of\nthe total number of civil helicopters produced that year. The-_^2neral Electric\nforecast is only for civil helicoptersweighing 10,000 pounds and over. The FAA\nforecast is for U.S. manufacturer civil output. Since each forecast excludes\nsome part of the total world civil helicopter output, each should be considered\nconservative. There is great variation in the forecast period. Forecasts for\nmore-than 6 or 7 years are usually considered to have the possibility of a wide\nmargin of error. The-G.E. forecast, the only forecast to 1990, suggests that\nannual production generally will increase linearly and will quadruple between\n1978 and 1990.\nU.S. MARKET\nThe annual world civil production of helicopters going to the U.S.\ncivil -helicopter market (Figure 2.2) had to be estimated since the data are not\ncollected directly. The U.S. civil helicopter market has been supplied by U.S.\nand foreign manufacturers. The number of civil helicopters produced by foreign\nmanufacturers for the American market has been recorded since 1964 by U.S.\ncustoms officials in the official U.S. import statistics \t\n\nU.S. Imports, Report\n\nFT226, under commodity number 6944030, U.S. Imports of Rotary Wing Aircraft,\nNon Military. Prior to 1965 data on helicopter imports were-not reported as a\nseparate commodity category.\nAn estimate of the number of civil helicopters produced by U.S. manufacturers for the U.S. market was made by subtracting U.S. civil, export of\nhelicopters from the total U.S. civil helicopter production. Except when there\nare large inventory changes the estimate will be quite accurate. The total\nF\t\n\nU.S. civil helicopter market is then estimated - by summing imports and the estimates of U.S. ` manufacturers. Range Unwell in Aesuspaliale lilbuglaph, 1171\nn 311 in 1911 Fleet Oileelnl Y\n\nT\n\xc2\xa3\n\n\t\n\ndiverse fleet of European-designed civil helicopters. The U.S. manufacturers\nmay also face stiff competition from Aerospatiale and joint European programs in\ntheir attempt to maintain their 20 percent share of the EEC countries\' military\nfleet (Figure 3.4). Certain European political actions impact the U.S, helicopter export market. These will be discussed in subsequent paragraphs.\nREST OF THE WORLD MARKET\nThis market seems to be the real plum in the pie for the next ten to\nfifteen years since it is iarger than the projected European market and considerably more "up for grabs" than either the European or North American markets. \t\ni\t\n\n^}\n\nThis market consists of the world civil and military helicopter markets\n`outside the U.S. and Europe and excluding Russian and Red China. Recent articles\njn aviation periodicals suggest that the U.S. has been performing well in this\n\nmarket; however, certain U.S. policies impact the competitive posture of U.S.\nmanufacturers against European manufacturers. Additionally, Aerospatiale exports\n\n8,0 percent of its\n\nproduction and a large number of these units go to this world\n\nmarket. Consequently it poses formidable competition for U.S. manufacturers,\nwhose combined export units fail to match those of Aerospatiale.\n}\t\n\nFOREIGN POLICIES IMPACTING U.S. COMPETITION\nFour tangibl e political actions were determined to constrain or\n\t\n\npotentially impact U.S. competitive posture in foreign helicopter markets.\n\nP\n\nThese are:\nr.\t\n\nt\n\nf\t\n\nimport duties\t\n\na\t\n\nembassy promotion\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\t\n\nlucrative government financing\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\t\n\njoint development programs.\n\nFigure 5.1 shows the customs duties imposed on civil helicopter\nimports by the countries of the major producers. There are no customs duties\non-military imports. The U.S. imposes a charge of 5 percent on the import\nvalue. All countries of the European Economic Community (EEC) have agreed to\na common external duty based on gross weight. The EEC imposes a charge of\n5 percent on the import value of helicopters weighing under 2,000 kilograms\n(4,409 pounds) and 15 percent on helicopters weighing 2,000 kilograms and over.\n\n5-4\n\nS\n\nPERCENT\'\nAD VALOREM\n\nn\n\n15\n\ny\nf\n\nt\t\nI\n\ng\t\',\n7\n\n>j\nin\n\nSOURCES. U.S. BUREAU OF THE CENSUS, U.S. IMPORTS FOA CONSUMPTION AND GENERAL\n=\nIMPORTS: \'TARIFF SCHEDULE OF U.S. ANNOTATE D, REPORT FT246 (WASHINGTON, D.C.: USGPO), ANNUALLY.\nTHE EUROPEAN ECONOMIC COMMUNITY, COMP0014 EXTERNAL DUTIES\n(BRUSSELS, BELGIUM: EEC). \t\n\n-\n\nFIGURE D.I. CUSTOM DUTIES ON CIVIL HELICOPTER IMPORTS, IWO\nk\t\n\n;\n\nk\n\nt\t\n\n\\\n\nSince almost all of the civil helicopters designed by European manufacturers\nhave a gross weight of over 2,000 kilograms, the European manufacturers are\n\nI\n\nreceiving considerable protection in their home markets.\nBecause helicopters are rather expensive items, the customs duty\nwill amount to a sizable dollar figure, e.g., a helicopter costing $400,000\nwould incur\'a duty charge of $20,000 at 5 percent or $60,000 at 15 percent.\nin _order for a foreign manufacturer to compete in the U.S. market, his product\n_\t\n\nmust be better or he must reduce his price enough below the domestic manufacturers to make up for the customs duty.\ni\n\nThe European helicopter manufacturers are aided in the export sales\n\nf\nI\n\nof their helicopters by their embassies throughout the world. This cooperation ranges from outright promotion to easing the prospective customer through\nnecessary red tape. By contrast, U.S. embassies are prohibited from promoting\nany aerospace products that are military arms or potential military arms.\nRecent European air transport sales have been characterized by very\nlucrative financing arrangements. The Eastern A300 Airbus buy and the Pan Am\npurchase of L1011\'s powered by Rolls Royce engines are cases in point. Minimal\ndown payments and long-term financing were underwritten by European governments.\nThis seems to be the European response to the Export-Import bank which has so\nfavorably served U.S. aerospace exports over the years. Whether or not\nEuropean nations make this lucrative financing available to prospective hel-icopte\'r purchasers or whether they plan to, is unknown.\nThe challenge posed by foreign competition is also intensified\n\nby\t\n\nj\n\nadvanced development programs. Britain and France joined together in 1967 on\nthe design, development, and production of three major military helicopter\nprograms; the Gazelle, Puma and the Lynx. These joint development programs\n-j\t\n\npotentially can result in an advanced technological endproduct due to the\npooling of R&D funds. In addition, these joint military programs virtually\nlock in substantial military orders from the sponsoring countries. The \t\n\n-^\t\n\nEuropeans can embark on joint programs in basic helicopter R&D. This was\nenacted when the Commission of European Communities prepared an "Action Programme for Aeronautical Research." Subsequently in 1977, a joint European\nresearch program in helicopter R&D was proposed.\n\n5-b\n\n?\t\n\nj\n\n:.l\nTiw\n\nI\ni\n\nU.S. POLICIES IMPACTING U.S. COMPETITION\nThe U.S. Government currently impacts helicopter exports in two\nways. They are known as pro rata reimbursement and bureaucratic delay.\nWhen exporting U.S.-produced. civil helicopters that are military derivatives\n\n(all U.S. civil helicopters fall into this category except the Bell 222 and\nthe Sikorsky S-76), the U.S. government levies a surtax on the purchase price.\n\nThe government recoups this fee as reimbursement of some of the R&D funds\nspent on the original military development program. \t\n\nPresent U.S. foreign\n\npolicy also causes delays in conducting foreign helicopter sales.\nWhen U.S. civil helicopters are to be exported, the Department of\nCommerce is involved in reviewing the sale. Similarly, military helicopter\nexports are reviewed by the Department of State. However, if U.S. civil\nhI elicopters are to be purchased by a country, or operators in a country, where\nhuman rights violations are alleged, both the Departments of State and Commerce\nreview the sale. These agencies not only cause delays in completing the sales,\nbut they also can veto the export sales. Apparently, neither the criteria used\nby these agencies to qualify a helicopter export sale, nor the legal authorization for their review functions are clearly specified.\nTECHNOLOGICAL REASONS FOR FOREIGN ADVANCES\nAny technologically advanced nation (such as the U.S.) aims at supply-\n\ning a substantial proportion of its domestic markets from domestic sources and\naims at as high a share of foreign markets as it can reasonably achieve. This\nis especially true of military markets, for obvious_-reasons, --and emphasizes\nthat there are strong political and economic pressures tending to influence\n\nmarket developments toward such a pattern.\nThe U.S. emerged from World War II in a commanding position in both\nmilitary and civil aviation. For many years, foreign manufacturers of helicopters were heavily dependent on U.S. technology and know-how, and licensing\nagreements for the manufacture of U.S. designs by foreign manufacturers were\ncommon. However, the market forces that shaped U.S. policy over the long term\nare now operating with less restraint among other well-developed nations, and\nGovernments are satisfying their military requirements increasincly with domestically-designed machines, at the same time seeking to <benefit from their\n5-7\n\n\t\n\nr\n:\'aia\n\nffi\nh\n\nexport. Moreover, some European helicopters designed-exclusively for the civil\n_market are proving remarkably successful, confirming the importance of developing products tailored to their intended market. Many of the latter machines\nincorporate some technologically innovative features of high apparent promise.\nIn-the field of advanced technology, France has long been recognized\nfor innova tive advances in aviation. The Alouette was the first helicopter\nti\t\n\nto be fitted with a gas turbine engine. A family of Aerospatiale machines is\nnow on the market (the Gazelle, Dauphin, Puma,and more recently the Ecureuil\n(AStar)) incorporating plastics and composite material rotor blades coupled\nwith design simplification. These machines combine economy of operation with\n\n7\n\nclaimed.,reliability and long life. The popularity of twin-engine foreign\ndesigns stems from the additional safety element,, and has undoubtedly influenced\nthe two new U.S. machines designed expressly for the civil market - the Bell 222\nand Sikorsky S-76.\n1\n\nU.S. emphasi s on military helicopter production during the Vietnam Mar\n\nJ\n\nwas of some advantage to foreign manufacturers in improving their share of the\nworld civil helicopter market, though such developments would have materialized_\n.,\n\nanyway as the respective national helicopter industries "matured" \t\n\n`\n\nsignificant that the first two American responses to the civil market situation,\n\nIt is\n\nthe Bell 222 and the Sikorsky S-76, are somewhat heavier and more expensive than\nt\n}\n\nthe European competition (with the exception of the Puma). \t\nr\n^a\n\nThe more recent European helicopters are undoubtedly attractively styled.:\np\t\np\t\nally\nOther things being equal, a machine with an attractive appearance possesses an\nobvious sales advantage. \t\n\nWhatever the performance advantages and disadvantages\n\n1\n\nof the "Fenestron" tail\t rotor, it is a distinctive feature of several Aerospatiale\na\n\nmachines, and serves to. , attract customer attention.\t\n\nIn the long term, markets will go to manufacturers that can offer\t\n_machines with desirable performance at competitive cost, coupled of course\nwith reliabili\n\nty and servicing support.\t\n\nTable 5.1 provides a comparison of\n\nhelicopters currently or shortly available in the general\t aviation market in\nthe U.S.\t in the 3,000 lb and up category.\t\n\nIn addition to initial\t cost,\t per-,\n\nformance criteria are listed, together with parameters in the last three columns\nin which carrying capacity is related directly to initial \t cost.\t\ns\n\nThe usual\n\nqualifier must be made that data from so many different sources is not necessarily\nstrictly comparable.; however, some general inferences may be drawn.\n\nr\n\n5-8\n\nl\n\nThe first and perhaps most obvious fact is that no two machines are\ndirectly competitive, as would occur for example if V;,!o machines having nearly\n\nf\n\nidentical performance were offered at a nearly identical\t price.\nThe Aerospatiale machines, the MBB BO-1055, and the Agusta 109A are\n;i\nat least interesting alternatives to the U.S. machines on offer. \t\ni\n\nThe modern\n\nAerospatiale machines are fast and attractively priced in spite of the U.S.\nj\t\n\nimport duty.\t\n\nThe MBB helicopter is twin-engined and has a long range.\t\n\nThe\n\nAgusta 109A is also a twin, and has the highest cruise speed of all.\nj\n\nThe Sikorsky S-76 and Bell\t 222 are heavier machines than the foreign\n\n\'Competition (with the exception of the Puma), although the Dauphin 2 matches\nthe Bell\t 222 fairly closely on cost and performance.\t\n\nThe first U.S. machines\n\nesigned specifically for civil \t operations are deliberately aimed at the business and offshore oil markets. \t\n\nIt may be anticipated that future U.S. designs\n\nwill materialize to meet more directly the inroads into civil markets that are\nbeing made by the lighter helicopters.\n\nLL.\n\n:\n\n\t\n\nE\n\n5-9\n\n_..\t _\t\n\n_s\n^\n\nv\n\n3k\n\n1\n\nl\n\nVI.\t\n\nROTORCRAFT-R&D FUNDING\n\nEuropean helicopter R&D funding has been estimated at running comprise a sizable position of Aero-\n\nspatiale \' s business\n\n(24 percent of pretax profits) this 5-10 percent\n\nshare is\n\nprobably representative of the internal funding of Westland MBB and Agu.sta ass\nwell.\t\n\nTable 6..1 shows summary funding figures for proposed EEC aeronautical\n\nresearch for helicopters and convertible aircraft.\nj\n\nThe two primary sources of rotorcraft R&D in SOURCES OF INFORMATION\n\ni\n\n_!\n\nC\t\n\nThere seem to be three reliable sources of information on foreign\nhelicopter markets and technology competition. They are the Army\'s Foreign\nScience and Technology Center (FSTC), the U.S. manufacturers\' task force,\n\n^\t\n\nand aviation periodicals. Interavia, flight International and Aviation Week\n\nC\t\n\nand Space Technology are particularly useful\n\nI\t\n\nr\t\n\n]\t\n\nSignificant market data may be stored on the NASA Aviation Data Base\nto which OAST and the NASA research centers have on-line access.\n\nThe data\n\nj\t\nfr\n\nin this data base can be updated and hard copy plots of significant parameters\nof interest can be outputted.. Appendix B consists of a memo which describes\nthe application and utility of the Aviation Data Base for aviation market and\ntrend analyses. ORI developed a user\'s guide and other documentation which can\np\t\ny\t\n\n.\t\n\nbe made available if the Program Office desires it. \t\n\nL\nI\n\n7-1\nt\n\nw\n\n\t\n\xe2\x80\xa2f\t\n\na\n\n1\n\n;\nt\n\nf\n\nVIII. CONCLUSIONS\n\ns,\n\ni\n\nWhile it is desirable to improve the USA position relative to foreign\ntechnology and foreign competition, it is necessary to recognize that the ad\n\ni\nvances made by European helicopter manufacturers in particular are the result\nof a sustained effort to secure a "share" of world markets, and that this\ns\t\n\neffort may be expected to continue into the indefinite future. The market is\n"maturing, and the realistic objective for the U.S. is to maintain a strong\n\nt;\t\n\nrelative position while accepting that the competition is here to stay.\nin the technological field, there is no evidence that foreign competition poses a significant threat. Provided funded research continues into\n\ni\n\ninnovative applications of new materials, construction techniques, and applications, no disadvantage in this area is likely to surfal:e. American manufacturers-are maintaining their lead in developing advanced and reliable\nmachines with a judicious blend of proven techniques and innovation.\nThe aggressive effort made by Aerospatiale to promote sales of light\n\nt\t\nt!\n\ntl medium helicopters in world civil \xe2\x80\xa2 markets bears watching\xc2\xb0 Provided U.S.\nmanufacturers are prepared to develop machines s p ecifically for the civil\nmarketwhich are somewhat smaller than the Sikorsky S-76- and the Bell 222 as\n\nfollow-ups to\n\nthese machines,, the\n\nfuture\n\ndoes not appear to be threatened.\n8_1\n\nmarket for the products\n\nof\n\nU.S. industry\n\ni\n_\n7\n\nThe Sikorsky compound research aircraft (RSRA) is an excellent example\nof U.S. technological \t know-how coupled with an innovative approach to research.\nIt is important that the astute promotion that is proving so successful \t in\nMarketing European products should be appreciated by U.S. \t sales personnel\t in\nmarketing their own machines. \t\n\ni\t\n\nThe U.S., too,\t is technologically advanced and\n\n-\' more emphasis should be placed on the skill with which such advances are combined into a solid background of experience to develop marketable products.\ni\n\nIn one field alone, Europe appears to offer a competitive edge.\nEurope\'s helicopters are attractive to look at. \t\n\nAs was the case some years\n\nago in the auto industry, the U.S. could benefit from a fresh assessment of\nthe role of "style" and "line" in the attraction a machine holds for a potential\ncustomer, and here the European example appears to have much to offer.\n\nl`\n\nE\n\nF\n\n\'\n\nE\t\nE\n\n8-2\n\nr\n\nr\t\n\n_\n\n.a.\n\nj\n\nh.\n\nTABLE A.1\nU.S. MANUFACTURERS\' HELICOPTER PRODUCTION\nCIVIL AND MILITARY\n(000\'s CURRENT. Kaplan\n\nSUBJECT:\t\n--\t\n\n.\t\n\nRollins\n\nAviation Data Base Development and Application - Commercial\nAircraft and Engine Shipments, U.S.-and Worldwide.\n\nThi s memorandum presents an example of the potential utility of the\nBasically, the system can be used to\nAviation Data Base to OAST analysts.\t\nOnstore data which is considered valuable for analysis of OAST programs.\t\nline data allows rapid access to reliable information, therefore, reducing\ntime spent on research and providing data for quick reaction tasks.\nThe way in which this system can be used was illustrated in a previous memorandum (May 26, 1976 Progress Report) where the demand for agricultural aviation was projected through the year 2000.\t\nThe necessary data. was\ninto\no\nt^1e tana fyss.\nwalfth "ur utility\tthe\n\t demonstrate\t\nAnother\t example\t has been\t developed to\ne\ns\n\n`\t\n\n.\n\nsystem.\n\nt\n\nOn June 11, 1976, ORI prepared a report for NASA Headquarters on\nworldwide and U.S. shipments of commercial aircraft for NASA\'s budget advocacy\nBoth past data and future projections were provided. \t\nMost of this\npackage.\t\ndata was compiled by hand from government and industrial sources.\t\nNone of the\nHad\nnecessary data was in the Aviation Data Base or the GEMAP Data Bases.\t\nthe \'Aviation Data Base been operative at that time, as it is now, that analysis\ncould have been done in a fraction of the time it did take.\nIn the report the data was separately grouped by: \t\n1) commercial\ntransports, 2) general aviation aircraft, both fixed wing and helicopters\nand 3) aircraft engines.\t\nThe data was further developed by ORI for entrance\ninto the data base.\t\nThe three groupings were changed into eight variables\n(see Table 1), four for U.S. manufacturers and four for worldwide shipments.\nAll\t eight variables were entered into the economic _data-file of the Aviation\nEach variable covers two ten year periods, 1966 - 1974 and 1975 Data Base.\t\n1986.\t The first period contains actuals and the second projected data. \t All\ndollars are stated in 1975 constant dollars.\n\nE\t\n\n4\n\nThe new variables were entered into the Aviation Data Base via the\nupdate program (see May 26, 1976 Progress Report) which prints out a complete\ndescription of each variable, including the source, time span, type and freHardcopied descriptions for each variable are provided in\nquency of the data. \t\nGraphic plots of the variables are presented in Figures 9 - 12.\nFigures \'1 - S.\t\n\n`\t\n\nCalculations were made with the system to determine total U.S. manufacturers aircraft shipments and total worldwide aircraft shipments for each\nThis was done by simply adding together the four variables associated\nyear.\t\nFigure 13 presents a graphic plot in time series\nwith each respective total.\t\nformat of the resulting calculation_.\n\nB-2\n.....rt\n\n._\n\ni.,\nAviation Data Base Oevelopment and Application - Commercial Aircraft and\nEngine Shipments, U.S. and Worldwide.\n\t\n(\'.\n\nJune 17, 1976\nPage 2\n\nn\nA major deficiency of the current system was uncovered when entering\nthe new variables. The update program allows the user to enter sources and\ndescriptions of the variables, however, only two lines of characters may be\nentered. Thus detailed descriptions and numerous sources cannot be entered\ninto the descriptive listing. This problem should be corrected in order to\nattain maximum utility from the system.\ny\n\n;l\n\n1\ni\ni\n\nJ\n\n[a\n\nB-3\n\nr^\na.\n\n4\n\nTABLE 1\nAVIATION DATA BASE\nVARIABLES FOR COMMERCIAL
